## 【英文原文】
# This Past Weekend: w/ FDNY Firefighter Tony Bonfiglio (Transcript)

Editor’s Notes: In this powerful episode of This Past Weekend, Theo Von sits down with Tony Bonfiglio, a legendary retired FDNY firefighter who spent 20 years serving the communities of Washington Heights and Queens. Tony shares gripping stories from his decades of service, including his firsthand experiences as a first responder during the tragic events of 9/11. The conversation also delves into the lighter side of firehouse life, from intense “oil fights” among recruits to the unique camaraderie and humor that sustained them through difficult shifts. Whether discussing the physical toll of firefighting or the personal journey chronicled in his book Tales from the Tiller, Tony provides a deeply human look at the life of a New York City hero. (Feb 4, 2026)

TRANSCRIPT:

## Meeting a New York Legend

THEO VON: Today’s guest is a retired firefighter and a veteran of the FDNY here in New York City, which is where we filmed. He spent 20 years serving with the fire department in the communities of Washington Heights and Queens and bravely served alongside many others during 9/11. I’m very grateful for his time and his service. He is what I would call a legend. Today’s guest is Mr. Tony Bonfiglio.

It’s too hot in here for you, Tony?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, I feel comfortable.

THEO VON: Okay. Yeah. What kind of temperature do you guys operate at?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, sometimes it’s so hot. In the summer when we’re out there in like 90 degree weather and you’re putting a fire out, it’s hot. You lose so much body water.

THEO VON: Yeah, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It’s like when you take off your turnout coat and your gear, it’s like you fell in a pool.

THEO VON: Have you ever started a fire where you had to pee and by the end you didn’t?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, yeah, that’s what I’m saying. Yeah. And then sometimes you’re so thirsty. I mean, there were times I was so thirsty from pulling ceilings and the plaster dust that I actually would look up and would take water coming off the drain pipe just right into my mouth because I can’t breathe.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. It gets pretty crummy in there. Oh, it’s shitty.

## Growing Up Italian in New York

THEO VON: 20 years you were, Tony Bonfiglio.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yep.

THEO VON: And that’s Italian.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s Italian, man. My whole family’s from East Harlem. Yeah, we’re Italian.

THEO VON: It’s fun, huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Grew up in New Hyde Park. My father moved us out of the Bronx when I was about 6.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And we moved to New Hyde Park. That’s like a town on the Queens borderline on the suburb side.

THEO VON: Bringing up New Hyde Park.

TONY BONFIGLIO: New Hyde Park. Went to Herricks High School.

THEO VON: What was it like back then?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, it was great. It was like all blue collar workers. All the blue collar workers’ kids. We had bus drivers, cops, firemen, truck drivers.

THEO VON: So it’s a nice suburb over there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, it was great. Right over the city line. I mean, I had such a great childhood. We had so much fun running around, doing all kinds of crazy sh*t back then. Hot rods, motorcycles, mischief.

THEO VON: Huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, mischief. Rock and roll, rock clubs. Oh, in Long Island rock clubs.

THEO VON: Listening to some Def Leppard, some AC/DC.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, well, back then it was Twisted Sister. We used to go to the clubs and see Twisted Sister. There was a bunch of good bands back then, house bands, but they would play all the cover songs. You had Doors. You had Zeppelin.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was awesome.

THEO VON: Yeah. That’s a beautiful time, dude. I think that’s a kind of a time that a lot of people romanticize as well.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I think so. I always say that I hope heaven’s like the 70s, man, because that was so awesome. Kicked a.

## The Path to Firefighting

THEO VON: And you were on the FDNY for 20 years.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, 21.

THEO VON: 21 years, yeah. How do you get started? Like, what were you doing before you got into firefighting? Because things are going well. You’re listening to Twisted Sister.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Hanging out with the boys, getting in trouble. I did get arrested. I was at Speaks club once in Lido Beach. And I did get arrested there for had some weed and a couple other things on us. Yeah, we were young, so yeah, we just. And I was going nowhere. I went to college for like maybe three weeks.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah. That’s not enough.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Farmingdale University. I went for food technology. I was going to be a meat inspector.

THEO VON: Oh, really?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, yeah. Thank God that didn’t happen.

THEO VON: Yeah. Hell yeah, dude.

## Working in the Meat Factory

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, I was working in a meat factory when I was a kid. Scraping hanger room floors like Paulie in Rocky. I had the white coat on, I’m freezing, scraping the blood and the fat off these floors all day.

THEO VON: And what was that like over there? So what kind of meats did they even have going in and out of there?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Just big sides of beef. Big factory, like hanging rooms. Big hanging rooms.

THEO VON: And would you be alone in there or you’d be up?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, there’d be other people. Butchers coming in, grabbing their meat. It was like a big production place. Big time. Lots of trucks. They would give all the meat out to all the restaurants and everything. And that was right in Mineola.

THEO VON: Oh, that’s pretty cool. And Mineola? Is that here in New York?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Mineola is pretty much right by New Hyde Park.

THEO VON: Got it. So you’re over there, you’re in there with the meat.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Like Rocky hitting the meat.

THEO VON: Yeah, I’m sure nobody’s looking because that.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Was right around the time, so.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. There we go. That’s what it looked like.

THEO VON: Oh, definitely. Everything turned to veal after this came out. Yeah, bro, you couldn’t get something that wasn’t tenderized.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was funny because we were all kids, too. And all this meat after the place closed would be. But we never took an ounce of meat.

THEO VON: You got all this beautiful meat, you guys.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It would be like big tubs of filet mignons, all kinds of things.

THEO VON: You guys are just in there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, yeah, yeah.

## The Cleanup Crew

THEO VON: And so what happens? Yeah, you’re in there. You guys are punching the meat or whatever. And is that a common, was that kind of a common future in your area?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, it was not at all. Just got a job there. My friends were all working there. The whole crew was working, and we were the cleanup crew. So once the butchers were all done, we would come in with these high pressure hoses, and we would just hose this whole place down.

THEO VON: Yeah, we’re just partying.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Then we’d oil it up with vegetable oil. It was a funny job.

THEO VON: What was the vegetable oil for?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Just make everything shiny and clean. Because we had the inspectors there. That was the two inspectors every day. And they would inspect everything. And if they didn’t get their payola, then they would knock a machine down, and then they’d have to rope the whole thing off, and we would have to come in and clean the machine, and then they inspect it again.

THEO VON: So it was a little bit of kind of a meat, was there kind of a meat mafia kind of going, a little bit mafia?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: Was it really? Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was owned by two Jewish brothers, the Cohens.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: So something was afoot. And so what makes you get out of there? Were your friends kind of graduating? Because I ended up working at, dude, me and like five of my buddies worked over there at, well, I think it was called Save a Center or something. It was a grocery or Winn Dixie, maybe it was called. Dude, one of my buddies would come clock in, go home, and then he would wake up, then he’d come clock out.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Dude.

THEO VON: He worked there for like, yeah, he worked there for almost 11 months.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Wow.

THEO VON: Dude. And the rest of us were afraid to do that. So we’d actually be in there working. But it was so much fun having your bro. There was nothing better, I think, than that time if you were either like late years of high school or right out of high school and you got to work with like your buddies who hadn’t gone, all like nobody kind of figured it out yet. You got to work with your friends.

## Oil Fights and Saturday Mornings

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s what it was. Saturday morning, we’d all be banged up from being out drinking all night. We’d just be getting home.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And we had to go in Saturday morning to clean, to oil the whole place. Oh, so those were fun trips. We’d have oil fights. Would get soaked in oil.

THEO VON: Oh, God. Well, I think we might pick up a new listener.

TONY BONFIGLIO: We would have some good oil fights.

THEO VON: Really? What?

TONY BONFIGLIO: We’d have these big squirt bottles, and then we’d have these 55 gallon drums of oil.

THEO VON: Yeah, it was seed oil.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was vegetable oil. Oh, we would even do the trucks in the vegetable oil. Looks like they got waxed. Yeah, they were all shiny.

THEO VON: And what vegetables was it coming out of?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I have no idea.

THEO VON: Look, a vegetable or what? I never even thought about that. What could even have that much oil? Maybe an eggplant. What is vegetable made of?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Vegetables, you think? But they squish them.

THEO VON: Vegetable oil is made from the oils extracted from various plant parts, like seeds, fruits, nuts, and grains. Most commonly soybeans, corn, canola, sunflower, and palm. Because that’s kind of the oil that everybody’s kind of against nowadays. But I guess you guys were just using it to keep stuff shiny.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Shiny.

THEO VON: Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: I didn’t know people use it like that.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, we had these black trucks and they would look all waxed after we were done oiling them all down.

THEO VON: Yeah.

## The Pit and the Freezer Men

TONY BONFIGLIO: And it stunk too, because it was such a big place. And there’d be a lot of like the bones and the fat. And you’d have to go in the pit. Sometimes it’s like you would puke, it was so bad smelling.

THEO VON: What was the pit? It was kind of like below the floor.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, that’s where all the water would drain into there. And every now and then our boss would have to go in there. I’d see him reaching in. He’d have like fat on his glasses. And it was, oh, it was gross.

THEO VON: Yeah. Oh, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. No, I didn’t want to stay there.

THEO VON: God, it was cold. It was cold in there too.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And there was freezers there. And the guys that worked in the freezers, you never saw them. They look like they were from the Antarctic. They had these hoods and these parkas and these big boots. And you’d see them now and then. They were kind of scary. You were a kid, you’re like, “There’s the freezer men.” They never came out of the freezer.

THEO VON: Yeah, dude. Oh, that’s f*ing wild. Yeah, be living in it. It’s hiding from your wife and kids probably in there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, God.

THEO VON: That’s when family life’s bad, when you’re like, “I don’t care how cold it is, I’ll stay in.”

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, yeah, that’s bad. That’s the last job you want is working in a freezer.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, I think so.

THEO VON: That was in the line. And what kind of guys would do it? Was it tough guys? Was it Russians?

## Working at the Meat Market

TONY BONFIGLIO: F*ing guys? Butchers, psychos. They got their own toolbox of knives. You know, the big. Oh, a couple of times they would pick you up by your shirt, you know, because we were just the kids, you know, we run it around.

THEO VON: Any ever get arrested for crimes or anything like that? Like, you think were any of them low key, like Dexters or like kind of that kind of guy?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, yeah, they were low key, but they were strange, some of them.

THEO VON: Yeah. Dang. That’s why.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Then you had the women. They were the packers. Oh, they were, yeah. They would like pack the chickens and all that and wrap it and everything.

THEO VON: Yeah, Women do better gift wrapping.

TONY BONFIGLIO: They had, like, tough women, too. They were working. It was like the boss of the women was a real tough broad.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, yeah.

THEO VON: Some girl would have tattoo of Richard.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Dent on us smoking. Everybody be out smoking. Yeah. Your smoke breaks and the guy would come with the roach and call you out for your coffee and sh*t.

THEO VON: He would.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, the coffee roach. You know, they were roach coaches here. Everybody would come out. You know what a roach coaches? It’s a coffee truck.

THEO VON: All right.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Back in the day, they would come to, like, factories.

THEO VON: Oh, so it just pulls up and you go and get your snack.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And they would be like, all right, the code guys here and everybody would be out a break, get their coffee, their lousy donut, whatever.

THEO VON: He had a little bit of a break. Yeah, I like that, man.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, dude.

## Kindergarten Memories

THEO VON: We stop. When I was a kindergartner, they had, or I don’t know what grade I was in. I wasn’t even in a grade, but I, but they, it was Nap time or whatever at kindergarten. But they would, I wouldn’t sleep right. I would keep my eyes open because they had this, they bring in this other lady to watch us, right? And I was, like, kind of curious about her, so I would just kind of lay over there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Eyeballing her.

THEO VON: Yeah, kind of eyeballing her, I guess, you know, because me and my mother always on the outs. I was shopping around, so I remember, like, at a certain point, she’d come over and kind of kick me a little bit and she’d let me go outside and watch her smoke.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s party.

THEO VON: Oh, that was nice, dude. And she had pretty nice hair. She looked a little bit like a man. Yeah, but she was definitely, she probably.

TONY BONFIGLIO: What year was that?

THEO VON: Oh, this was probably 84, early 80s.

TONY BONFIGLIO: God.

THEO VON: Yeah, she looked like a man, but she’s pretty. She’s like only the third woman I’d ever seen, you know? So at that point, she was really good looking.

TONY BONFIGLIO: You don’t know.

THEO VON: Yeah, yeah, she’s beautiful to me, you know, she was dying, you know, she was just stunning, dude. But, yeah, she’d let me go out there and watch her smoke and she’d complain about stuff.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, that’s funny.

THEO VON: God, that was nice. Yeah, Just getting a little break, you know. So that was like, it felt like it was my break from kindergarten. Like, we’re on break.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, I remember kindergarten well.

THEO VON: Really?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, yeah. Nap time. That was the best.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Snack and nap and then out to the sandbox and dig a hole of China.

THEO VON: Yeah, dig a hole down to a butcher shop.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, they would always put like a cone in the sand. They’d be like, oh, you’re almost in China. You know, I’ll be like, yeah, let’s keep digging.

## From the Freezer to the Fire

THEO VON: Yeah, dude. And then some kid would get sand in his eyes and he would look like he was Chinese. Yeah, yeah, that’s. Yeah, that’s what I got. Good, dude. So, yeah, so take me out. How do you get out of the meat area into the, into the fire? Like, take me out of the freezer. Enter the fire, man.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, I, I went, I went before. After that, I went to a plastic mold injection factory job.

THEO VON: Did you?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Unemployment sent me to.

THEO VON: Oh, they did. They were, they were making body parts.

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, no, we were making, there was a game called Othello back then. It was like a white.

THEO VON: Yeah, remember that game?

TONY BONFIGLIO: And we were making the chips and we made the colored beads. So it was all like these plastic mold injectors.

THEO VON: The color beads. Like what, Mardi Gras or something?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, or people just buy them for their crafts and stuff like that. All they’re like 100 different colors.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And I could never remember the color after I backed, boxed it up and get ready to send it back up. I’d be like, oh, amethyst, brown, green. I put something down, and then the guy would come back. You can’t be. Okay, be yelling at me. You can’t send these out. And I’m laughing. He’s like, what are you laughing about? I mean, I’m making $3 an hour, dude.

THEO VON: I remember one time I ate a bunch of mushrooms or whatever after school.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: And I worked at this mail center. Right. My job was to mail out these insurance forms to these different companies around the country. Well, I’m in the mail room, dude. I’m not doing real good.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, I could imagine.

THEO VON: Yeah. Well, my body had gotten really hot, so I took all my clothes off and I, put all my clothes into a box. Mailed, mailed them to somebody.

TONY BONFIGLIO: My God, that’s great, dude.

THEO VON: My first girlfriend, her mom got me that job. Her, her dad, shout out, Mr. Earl. He was a fire chief, actually.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, really?

THEO VON: Yep.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And.

THEO VON: But anyway, I got laid off. And, yeah, the mother had to come. Thank God it was a day, it was raining because she let me borrow a raincoat she had so I could go get in my car.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Wow.

THEO VON: So anyway, we made some tough choices over the years, but, but I did have, have a good time. They were state, they had a firehouse down there on Chapatoulas in New Orleans. And it would be great because the Mardi Gras parade there. So we go down there, and the firehouse would be open on days like that and all.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Must have been an old firehouse, too. It was beautiful. Yeah.

THEO VON: I think. Bring it right up. It’s right down there. Off a chop of toolis over there. And that was it right there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, I see it.

THEO VON: That’s it.

TONY BONFIGLIO: They made it into something.

THEO VON: Yeah, they made it into something.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Wow.

THEO VON: But yet, anyway, it was a great time. And it was a great time. We go over there, everybody’s cooking hot dogs.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: And just, you know, just having a great time, you know. That was a beautiful time.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, the Cajun.

THEO VON: Yeah. We had so much fun.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I’ll bet.

## The Path to Becoming a Firefighter

THEO VON: So how do you get into there, man? How do you get, so you’re, you’re over there making jewelry and stuff.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, the funny part is they sent me to this place called Stonewell Plastics in Mineola. And I go in and I got this little Mexican guy, he’s doing the interview with me. He’s got, it’s like, middle aged little guy, he’s got a little pencil mustache. So I’m sitting there, I’m 18, you know. And he goes, so, what’s your name? And I said, Tony Bonfiglio. He writes it down. He says, and how old are you, Tony? I said, I’m 18. Writes it down. He goes, Antonio, were you born here? I said, no, I was born in New Jersey. He starts laughing. Oh, oh, you’ll do. You’ll do. And when I went back there, I realized I was the only American in the place. Everybody else was foreign.

THEO VON: Oh, damn. So you’re in there learning Spanish, huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, Spanish.

THEO VON: What languages were they? Yeah. Who was.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Who was mostly Spanish? Yeah, yeah, there was some black people, but, but even them I didn’t understand because some of them from Brooklyn. I had one friend, he would drive me home. I would say, yeah, I’d shake his head and he’d shake his head. And we didn’t know what the hell Eli was saying.

THEO VON: Oh, dude, I still can’t understand.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I’d be like, hey, Ms. Salvatore, steal my damn boss having. I’ll be like, yeah, yeah, yeah, dude. I mean, look, bro, I don’t know what he said.

THEO VON: It’s 40 years later, something’s never changed and maybe it’s for the best, you know?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Could be. Yeah.

THEO VON: So you, so you’re in here. Are you just like this in from me or how do the winds, how do the winds blow you over to the fire world?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, then I went, my father got me into the printing union, the Amalgamated Lithographers of America, Local 1. Wow. And I did that for about four years, but I took the fire department test when I was 18. I was 19 in like 78.

THEO VON: And was that part of school you had to take it?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, my neighbor came over and he came in our back door with an application. Johnny Lalima. Thank God. Johnny saved my life. Gives me the application. He says, you’ll never get rich on this job, but he’ll put a roof over your head and food on your table. So I’m sitting at my kitchen table, I’m like, okay, Johnny, thanks. You know, I had no idea it was going to be the biggest career move of my life.

THEO VON: Dude. What? Yeah. What made him even come over there and do that on one night?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I guess he knew I was going nowhere because he was my neighbor and they saw us all hanging out all the time, you know? Yeah, well, you figured this kid be a fireman. It’d be all right. So thank God. I mean, he saved me because I was going, the printing union was going south. You know, computers were coming in.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And, you know, they weren’t printing anymore on these big printing presses, so, so I got that. And then about a year later, I took a physical test after the written test. The written test we took in a high school somewhere in Queens.

## Taking the Fire Department Test

THEO VON: And was there a lot of people taking those tests at the 40,000. 40,000 people wanted to be firemen.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: Because the job, I think in, in a lot of ways it’s a very family, like, it’s a, like it’s a lineage job. It’s like a lot of families do it, definitely, and there’s a lot of esteem with it, you know, especially at that time. What was it like? Like, was it a very revered position? Did you even think you could get it?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Get the job? Yeah, yeah, I think I, you know, because I was 19, I was in great shape. And the physical test is really where they separate everybody. The written test was a joke. I mean, it was like a third grade questions, you know?

THEO VON: Yeah. Remember any of them or, no.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, I, you know, I saw the, the sanitation guy, he was saying about the dirt and the shovel, that’s exactly what it was. Like, what would you use, a garbage truck, a plow, or a shovel and brew. But it’d be ridiculous if you got your name right, you know, that was it. So I got a 98 on the written test, probably the highest I ever gotten any test in my life.

THEO VON: But what was the physical part like?

## The Physical Test and an Unexpected Zero

Like, my father took me to East New York to an armory in this real sh neighborhood. And he parked it. We parked his Buick Regal. It was like the nicest car my dad ever had. And I bought him these spoke rims for it for his birthday.

THEO VON: Nice.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So we get there and he says, here, you take the keys, I’m running for the subway and you take the car home. I’m like, yeah, you sure, dad? You want to. Yeah, don’t worry about it. I mean, he’s an East Harlem guy, so I wasn’t too worried about him.

So he took off. I go in this armory. It’s like when you get in there, it’s the size of a football field. And there’s 100 guys that day that are going to take the test. And they break you up into like 10, 10 men groups. And you go around all these different stations and you take different tests. You had to run. One was a mile, one was an eight foot wall. You had to jump over the eight foot wall. That was like the separator. Like if you didn’t get over the eight foot wall, you went to the police department. Sorry, guys. But hey, that’s what separated them.

THEO VON: That’s what it is, man. Sometimes you got to separate the beef from the pork, you know.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, the funny part is I got a zero on one of them. And if you get a zero on one of those stations, you’re done. You’re not going to get hired.

THEO VON: Yeah, for sure. Which one was it?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I had this thing called a ledge walk. So you had to put on a turnout coat, a helmet, you had to put boots on, you had to put a mask on. And you went up on a balance beam next to a wall and you had to slide along the wall. Like you were shimmying along a ledge. It was called a ledge walk.

I’m shimmying along this thing. I’m like, why the f* would I be on a ledge? I mean, is this job that crazy? One of my Batman, you know, I’m going to be out on the ledge? And I went all the way down, I touched the line, I came all the way back and the woman scoring me says, you didn’t touch the line down there. What? I raced all the way back, touched the line, I come back, she ran out of time. I got a zero. I was like, ah, that’s it. Everything was down the drain.

THEO VON: So come on, you think she just didn’t do it correctly?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I think, yeah, I do. I think she was there to knock some of the white guys out because there was too many white guys on the job, which they were complaining about. They wanted women, they wanted minorities. I think they were told because I stepped on the line. She wasn’t even anywhere near the line.

THEO VON: No.

TONY BONFIGLIO: But you know, it’s like everything happens for a reason, you know, I mean, right.

## The Lawsuit That Changed Everything

THEO VON: And so you get the zero, pissed. And so but, and there’s 10 stations you do. Fine on the rest of them. You get out of there and are you then waiting for your grade? Like, do you even like…

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. So now, now after you get all that done, everybody says their half-assed goodbyes, you know. I went back out, thank God my father’s Regal was still there. Yeah, found it. I got into Regal. I found I had a joint in my workout bag. I lit that sucker up and now I’m driving home. We had no directions back then. So I’m looking for signs for the LIE. And boom, get home. My dad was there. How’d you do? I said, yeah, I think I did all right.

And so now the girls have a lawsuit because 40 girls took the test, the first 40 girls ever, and they didn’t pass. With 40,000 applicants, you know, none of them passed. None of them passed.

THEO VON: Were some of the women in there on that day you were there with that 100 people?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, I didn’t see any women that day, but we had a couple in my battalion when I got there. But so anyway, they had a lawsuit and this lawsuit went on for six years. So from that time I took the test, I didn’t get a notice that I was hired for six years because it was because of the women. Because the lawsuit took so long.

They usually hire about 2,500 people off the list. Now they had to go deep into the list. And because of that zero, I was like 4,300 on the list. So the women saved me. Thank God. Thank you, girls. I appreciate it.

THEO VON: Yeah, because rarely does a complaining woman save you.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, they saved me that day.

THEO VON: Brenda Berkman right there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Berkman. Everybody knows Brenda.

THEO VON: Pioneering female firefighter. She was the sole name class. She was a lawyer in the federal sex discrimination lawsuit that opened the fire department city in New York to women firefighters. After she won the lawsuit in 1982, she and 40 other women became FDNY firefighters. Was that a time where, like, were people supportive of the women? Were they against the women? What did that feel like?

TONY BONFIGLIO: They were totally against the women.

THEO VON: They were.

TONY BONFIGLIO: There’s an all male place, you know.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And you know, I guess a lot of guys, they don’t feel like they didn’t mind the women that really passed the test. But to just get on because you’re a woman, that was not, you know, today, now they have girls. You know, there’s so much into athletics and stuff. They could pass these tests now, but back then, you know, it wasn’t like that.

THEO VON: So some of it, they were just kind of stacking the deck like we’re just going to put some extra girls in because some of that’s just a liability.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s it. They want you. Well, yeah, life and death, you know.

THEO VON: And a liability for their own life.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That too. Yeah. But I mean, if your kid is trapped in a fire, you know, you want the best person going to get that kid. Not, you know, somebody that didn’t make it.

THEO VON: Yeah, for sure.

TONY BONFIGLIO: 100%.

THEO VON: I agree.

TONY BONFIGLIO: But they saved me. And I’m the luckiest guy for that.

THEO VON: So you’re in, huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I’m in.

THEO VON: You made it in.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

## Getting the Letter

THEO VON: And do you remember like, like when you do get a letter that’s like, yeah, I’m in, guy. You got it. And does it have flames?

TONY BONFIGLIO: My wife came. I come pulling up in the parking lot from working at Mastercraft Litho, and I had a little Chevette. We both shared this little Chevette car that my wife bought on her own when we were kids. And I see her coming across the parking lot waving. I said, what is it? She goes, “Fire department watch. Six years. Fire department watch. They’re going to hire you.” I said, holy sh. I looked at it. Yeah. Tony Bonfiglio, New York City fireman, report to Randall’s Island. 8/23/84.

THEO VON: Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I’ll be there.

THEO VON: That’s pretty cool, huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh my God. So happy.

THEO VON: Oh, well, she was happy.

TONY BONFIGLIO: She was happy. I was happy.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: They’re coming down with one of my friends and he’s like, hey, these two girls just moved in from the Bronx, you know, Chris and Bernie. And I’m like, hey. First of all, I thought he was pulling my leg. But so that was like why we met and then, you know, we went through school. We didn’t really date until we were like 18.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s a great shot. Yeah, I was like size 32 waist back then.

## Assignment to Ladder 34

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, the funny part is they give us your assignment, you know, so I get this thing, 34 truck. Now like 150 firemen, only 10 are going to go to trucks. The other 140 are going to go to engine companies. There’s an engine company, they’re the water. They got the hoses and then there’s the truck. That’s the ladders. And you know, they break down the doors, the ledge walkers.

So I didn’t want to go to a truck. I was like real. I didn’t like the heights, I didn’t like the ropes.

THEO VON: Yeah, look, I got a zero on the ledge walk. Yeah, you got the wrong guy. So, and why did they choose you for that then?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I have no idea. My father said they saw how good I was at breaking things, that they said, send him to the truck. So now I see a truck in Manhattan, I’m like, oh man. All right. So I tell her, I says, I got, I’m going to a 34 truck. They gave me a truck. She’s like, they gave me a truck. I’m like, yeah. Because most guys are in the engine for like maybe five, ten years before they get to a truck.

THEO VON: And so in the engine means they’re in the actual fire engine.

TONY BONFIGLIO: They’re in the engine and they’re a separate company. You know, their engine A4. And we were at a 34. It was a big hundred year old firehouse.

THEO VON: And so a ladder, they do different stuff than the engine does.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, the engine puts the fire out.

THEO VON: Okay.

TONY BONFIGLIO: The ladder opens up, opens up the door, breaks the doors down, opens, cuts the roofs open, makes all the rescues and the searches. It’s a lot more intimidating because you don’t have the hose line. You’re actually in there crawling around with no water. No water. Maybe a can on your back.

THEO VON: Oh, what?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, I was a can man for quite a while.

THEO VON: And that’s heating up quick, probably.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: So you get in a ladder and where’s the ladder at?

TONY BONFIGLIO: 161st Street in Manhattan, off of Amsterdam Avenue in a place called Washington Heights.

THEO VON: Washington Heights?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: Yeah. A lot of Dominicans up there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh my God. When I got there, they were just coming in. Yeah, and they took over with blood, man. They killed everybody.

THEO VON: Really?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, they took over the drug business because that was the hub. You had the GW Bridge right there. You had all the parkways to head out to North Brooklyn, out to Long Island. So everybody would come in and buy their crack and their cocaine.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So it was badass. I mean, they would be shooting all day long.

THEO VON: Was it exciting?

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was, yeah. Oh my God. And the place was so crowded. It was like full of people with these old broken down tenement buildings, brownstones, tenements, all broken down, hundreds years old. The firehouse was 100 years old.

THEO VON: Did you, was it exciting to go work there? Did it feel scary? Like, what did it kind of become?

## First Assignment and Finding the Firehouse

Well, the funny part I was going to say is that I went with my wife from the graduation. I said, we got to go. It’s on 84th Street. I told her I didn’t realize that was 84 engine when I read the thing. So I take her into Manhattan and I take her down 84th street and there’s no firehouse. Finally, I go around 85th street and I find a firehouse and I got my uniform on. And they tell you to not always knock on the door and say, probery, probationary firefighter Bonfiglio. This old guy answers the door, what can I do for you, probie? He knew right away I was a probie just by looking at me with the stuff on. I said, I’m looking for my Firehouse 34 truck. They said it’s on 84th Street. He’s like, why? Let me look at that. He’s like, dude, you’re in with 84 engine and you’re on 161st Street. I was like, why? Holy. I had to get in the car and tell my wife, we got to go up the water. And now we left the glitz and now we’re in this freaking rundown neighborhood. It was like, holy.

## The First Real Fire

TONY BONFIGLIO: My first fire. Well, I’ve been like car fires, rubbish fires. We had water leaks, so many water leaks. Because it was such an old neighborhood. You know, when we go to a water leak, we got to find out where it’s leaking. Sometimes we got to break into the apartment, gas leaks. We did everything, all the utilities up there.

So I doing it for about three weeks. I’m still wondering, oh my God, what’s going to happen with a job? Am I going to. What’s it going to be like? I still have no idea. You know, I’m a can man. The proby gets the can.

THEO VON: Okay. And the can means what?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I got a fire extinguisher with a strap on my back and I got a hook. And I’m going to be the guy that if I can put out whatever fire I can with the can.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It’ll put out. If a good can man could maybe put out a room of fire.

THEO VON: Okay. Okay.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It’s real. Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was like three weeks in and I’m doing my first 12 by watch. So you have a house watch, you know, and you got a man. Somebody’s got a man. It. It’s got a computer in there that comes on and tells you what your alarms are and you acknowledge them. You hit all the lights, you send the companies out. So I got to do my first 12 by. So I never done a watch alone yet. So the 12 buy was like, you know, it was a little scary at first.

THEO VON: And that’s 12 hours.

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, 12 to three, you do three hour watch.

THEO VON: Okay.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So 12 o’clock came, I went in, I took the book over. It’s midnight and I’m sitting there and it’s like one o’clock in the morning and all of a sudden like 1:30, I start falling asleep. I’m like sleeping.

THEO VON: Look, I’ve had a job before. I know how it is.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I’m sacked out. All of a sudden the alarm goes off. The. The computer goes. It starts ticking out, this alarm. I’m like, oh my God, I’m was so scared. I got up, I hit the housewatch light. I’m looking at the ticket. It says engine A4, ladder three four, first two, fire on an eighth floor.

So now I got to hit all the bunk room lights. I got to hit the intercom, say everybody goes. I got to hit the three bells. And then I got to acknowledge on the computer, both companies, 10, 4. And then I got to take the tickets and put them on the truck side and the engine side. And then I put my gear on and out the door we go.

## Into the Fire

And so we had a forceful entry team. Me, my lieutenant, who was this salty guy from the Bronx, Spinelli. He was like a real. I mean, he was the war days, you know, back in the 60s and the 70s.

So I’m like, I don’t know. So this might not be it. Take an elevator to the seventh floor, because the fire was on the eighth floor. So we get to the seventh floor and you never take the fire. The elevator to the fire floor for obvious reasons.

So we take it and we take the stairs to the eighth floor and we get in this hallway. And now it’s like a big projects hallway. I don’t know if you ever seen one of them, but they’re painted green and they got like fluorescent lights and it’s long. So we go to all the doors and we stick our noses in the jams trying to smell smoke. We don’t smell nothing.

So the boss says to my friend Jimmy the Duke, he says, go up to the next floor and check it out. Calls down the battalion, says, yeah, we got nothing showing on the eighth floor. We’re going to check out the ninth floor. So with that, now we go into the hallway and my friend is on the top of the stairs and he says, lou, I think we got something.

So I’m like, oh, shit, this is it. I’m in this project hallway, caught at four in the morning. I’m running up the steps with my hook and everything. And there I. And I said, the boss says, what do we have? And the Duke opens this door and I was like, oh, my God, if there was a gate to hell, this was it. Okay. Black shimmering smoke that looked like satin curtains just going in all different directions. And my first thought was, no way we’re f*ing going in there, right?

And he says, hit it with the can. Got the can. And I’m on my knees and I go to hit it and nothing comes out. I hit it again. Nothing comes out. I didn’t have air in the can. It’s like a total f* up for.

THEO VON: We’re in and out.

And I could feel, I’m under the bed, feeling around, trying to find this kid. And I get to a window. I break the window out, and I look out the window and I see my irons man that was with me. He’s in the street with the kid, a limp, limp kid, you know, and they’re taking the kid. They open the battalion car door. He throws, they go in there and they rushed off to Columbia Presbyterian.

THEO VON: How’d the kid get out?

TONY BONFIGLIO: No, he carried. She was unconscious. He found out she didn’t make it. She didn’t. The kid was dead. Unfortunately, she was burned. They tried to revive her at the hospital, but it didn’t work.

So I’m overhauling now, and I see my lieutenant, and I said, “I just saw Jeff in the street with the kid.” He’s like, “Anthony, the kid was right there behind the door.” And I was like, oh, I was crushed, man. I shut that door. I felt like I killed that kid, you know?

So we go to Presbyterian. Oh, so before we leave now, the deputy shows up because now we got a 1045, which is a body. And he’s like, “What happened here?” We’re in this apartment. People are still playing cards. So my lieutenant says, “Well, we came up to the apartment,” and I said, “I’ll tell you what happened.” And now the deputy looks at me, you know, I go, “You see these motherfers over here? I said, they didn’t bother to fing tell us there might be a kid in that back room.” And I’m throwing F bombs at him.

And the deputy says, “Lieutenant, you better control your man.” So the boss puts his arm around my shoulder. He says, “Come on, come on. I’m Finny.” Called me Amphony. That’s Anthony in a brogue. “Amphony, let’s go down to the street.” You know, we went down to the street and we went and we picked up my friend Jeff at the hospital. I said, “How’d they make out with the kid?” He said, “They were trying to revive her. They were bringing her to the burn center.” So that was the last we heard of her.

THEO VON: But there’s no way you could have known that, right? Because you go in and the kid was hiding behind.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Who would think? You’d think somebody would tell us there could be a kid back.

THEO VON: It’s heartbreaking.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, man.

THEO VON: Do you ever find out why they wouldn’t let you know what was going on there?

## The Hidden Dangers of SRO Buildings

TONY BONFIGLIO: I guess there was like an SRO bid or something. They were renting out that little back room to this woman and a child, you know, so it was pretty bad. And I had a few of those. I mean, right after I got over, was getting over with that one, I had another one where we showed up without our masks on because it was the middle of the day.

THEO VON: Yeah, no, I get.

TONY BONFIGLIO: There was nothing showing. We get out and the guy says, “Hey, they’re working on the oil burner all day.” And we had a lot of oil burners, you know, they would smoke up. So we’re like 1020 says on the box, which means all the other companies, take your time. Don’t blow the red lights and s*.

So now we’re heading up to the stairs to the caller’s apartment. It’s forcible entry team. Me, Lieutenant Clipper, and this guy Jimmy Lynch. And we get to the door, and the woman opens the door and we own the apartment. And it’s like, like a slight smoke condition in there, but slight. So we’re like, “What’s what? What do we have here?”

So my boss says, he was a smart fireman. He was from rescue, too. He said, “I think we got something above us.” Usually it’s below us, you know, but this, I guess, because the light was, the smoke was so light. So now we get up. We don’t have no masks or nothing. We get to the, I see smoke coming out of the doorway, you know, the locked door in the hallway. So I turn around, I like, donkey kicked the door open, like, three kicks. It kicks open, and we got a black wall of smoke. We’re like, holy. Now we got, after the 1020, we got to give a 1075, which means now we have a fire. So now we’re crawling in.

THEO VON: No mask.

TONY BONFIGLIO: No mask. We’re on our bellies.

THEO VON: Was it better or worse without a mask in this?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Worse. It was worse.

THEO VON: Yeah, because you could feel the heat.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. You know, back in the day when they didn’t wear masks, like in the 60s and the 50s, everything was wooden, cotton. And then once the 60s and 70s, and everything’s vinyl and plastic, and it’s a whole different smoke. You know, it’s poisonous.

THEO VON: So it gets a great point, right?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Yeah. Back then, you could suck the cotton and the wood, you know?

THEO VON: It’s like, yeah, you’re probably, yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: You’re like, smoking a pack of cigarettes.

THEO VON: Or you’re like, hell, I wish somebody, I wish somebody had a couple tobacco plants in here. Crawl around for another two minutes.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Exactly. That’s what it was like.

THEO VON: Hence, things would burn slower back then, too, wouldn’t they?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, back then, yeah. But plastics will take off a lot faster.

THEO VON: God.

## A Tragic Discovery

TONY BONFIGLIO: So anyway, he crawls in, and we’re like, “Oh, s*, we got to go in.” Jimmy goes in ahead of him, and I’m the last guy in. We’re like, almost on our bellies, crawling in, and I hear the horrible words, “We got bodies.” I’m like, “Oh, my God.” Now we got to give 1045s. So we had 1020. That means don’t come. Take it easy getting there. Now we want 1075. Now we got two 1045s, two bodies.

So with that, I get a body, a little girl come to pass it back to me, and I cry, like, shimmy out of the apartment.

THEO VON: You don’t know if she, do you?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I don’t know any. I can’t see nothing yet. I still can’t see nothing until I get to the stairwell. I knew I had a kid in my arms. I see it’s a little girl, you know, the face is all darkened. So now I’m like, “Oh, s*.” I’m going to run her down to the EMS, is going to take her in the street. And I go running down the street, the street, and there’s nobody there. And all the people are screaming. You know, they’re yelling and screaming. And I’m holding this kid and I’m looking around because we gave the 1020, there was nobody there.

THEO VON: Yeah, they’re at the malt shop.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I put the kid down. I did CPR on this little girl and I, you know, I put it down, I cup her head and I, pretty much all I knew was like a 15 and 2. You know, you get two breaths into her and then you give her 15. And this went on for like, like 10 minutes before I got relieved.

And the sound that she was making when the air would come out, they call it machine gun breath because it’s like. And the smell, because I had the smell of the burnt lips. So finally somebody came over and they started taking over. And then the EMS came. After 10 minutes, they let me go. So now I’m like, I’m walking around the sidewalk. All these Dominicans are very emotional. They’re all screaming and yelling and crying and everything. Some of them probably praying and everything.

THEO VON: Yeah, for sure.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I figured, let me go back up to the floor because my, the other two guys were working on the mother. And when I got there, the EMS already took over. And I was like, “Holy s*.” And it was like the first fire we had where the windows didn’t break open. They were the new windows that the Gambino crime family put in in all of Manhattan. And they didn’t break like the old windows, so it never got air. So it smoldered. And they died from the smoke.

THEO VON: God.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And that stayed with me for years. I would like, sometimes I would smell and taste the burnt lips and everything and, ah, it was horrible.

## Processing Trauma

THEO VON: That’s heartbreaking. Like, who do you even talk to for about that kind of stuff? Do you guys go to some of the services? What is some of that like?

TONY BONFIGLIO: I got back in the firehouse, the guys were listening up. They knew I took my turnout coat off and I just went in, I took a shower. We all took showers. And then the boss says into the office, you know, “Let’s talk about it.” So he says, “You know, we did everything we could. It really wasn’t nothing else we could do. I mean, not having the mask didn’t hamper us at all because we still got the bodies.”

And so then I went home. My wife heard about it already on the news. And so when I got home, they all greeted me, and that was pretty much, you know, all I got was some nice hugs and some tears at home. Yeah. Yeah, you get over, you know, it takes a while, though.

I mean, I had a couple of things. I had this junkie that we kept, called three times. We got called to the sixth floor. He kept lighting fires to stay warm in the winter. Yeah, you know, he was a young black kid, you know, and so we would have to climb up these vacant stairs. I had to pop a hole in the cinder block walls to get in. Vacant building.

THEO VON: And a couple times it’s happened.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. Oh, well, it’s the first one. We go up, sixth floor. Dark, cold. It’s icy. It’s about 30 degrees out.

THEO VON: A nice kid.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I have no idea. I got to the back with my, and I see my Irish lieutenant yelling at him. He’s like, “What the f* you doing? You can’t light a fire.” Blah, blah, blah. And he was sitting there, and he was in the corner, and I came in, I put my flashlight on him, and he was looking at me with these eyes, you know. And I was like, “God, he had like, tombstones in his eyes,” you know? And he was only about 16, 17.

So we put the fire out. We leave two hours later, we get called back again. Go up. Got to climb these stairs. Six floors, no steps, just risers and no lights. It’s dark. So now we climb all the way up again. We go through the thing. He lit it up again. Bosses, “Yo, you can’t fing do this.” He’s yelling at him and s. I got the light on him and, and the funny part was we just had, we just broke into an Entenmann’s cake at the firehouse. Vanilla icing that we would keep in the fridge. Get it nice and stiff.

So as my friend Jeff, in the dark, he’s putting the fire out. I put the light on him, and he still got the vanilla icing on his mustache.

THEO VON: Somebody took a piece for the road. Jeff. Huh?

TONY BONFIGLIO: The boss is yelling at us.

THEO VON: That’s why he was pissed. This guy interrupted his dessert.

TONY BONFIGLIO: All three companies and a battalion are responding every time. And we got chains on. It’s snow, and it’s not like, no, right.

THEO VON: So you can see out there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So the third time I hear, we just, we just got back from something. We went in our bunks, and we get in the bunks, and we’re like a little pillow talk before everybody was sleeping. And then before you know it, beam, boom. Same box. Here we go again. I hear the boss yelling, “I’ll kill that dude.”

THEO VON: Let him cook.

TONY BONFIGLIO: At this point, we’re sliding down the pole.

THEO VON: This guy’s trying to get to heaven. And you guys keep, oh, my God, you guys keep coming in and ruining his trip.

## The Third Call

TONY BONFIGLIO: Third time. So we’re in the rig, and I said to my friend Jeff, I says, “Let’s tell the boss, stay on the, don’t even come up the stairs. We’ll take care of it.” And surprisingly, he agreed. So when we got to the door, I says, “Lou, we’ll take care of you. Stay down here.”

So me and Jeff go up though my light, my light. My die hard battery died on me, so I had like, no light left. I’m trying to follow Jeff’s light. And we’re going up just on the rises, and then there’s no platform. So you got to like, lean over your step to get on. And when you get to the fifth or sixth floor, you’re looking down at this skeleton staircase.

## The Heroin Addict’s Apartment

Yeah. So now we go in, go through the dark apartment again. He’s in there again. Now he’s got candles burning because we broke his bucket up, smashed it all up. So we’re like, holy. So now Jeff is like leaning into him, and he says, listen, you can’t do this anymore. And Jeff’s like the good cop, you know, I’m the bad cop.

So I lean into him, I look at him, I says, we come back here again, I’m throwing you out that f*ing window. And he just looked at me, you know, and that was it. That was the last time we went back. We left there, we took his candles.

But years later, I would see that kid’s face looking at me, and it made me feel so bad. A wasted life like that, you know, heroin, he had the works. Everything that’s scary.

THEO VON: And the fact that he kept going back to do it, it’s just that power of addiction, you know?

TONY BONFIGLIO: It was cold.

THEO VON: Oh, I guess they keep you warm.

TONY BONFIGLIO: He was in a vacant. Yeah. Oh, yeah. A lot of like that vacants were disgusting.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Squatters. Yeah, they’re on the floor. Drugs, needles. And you’re crawling around in there because you still have to save them. Oh, yeah, yeah. Vacant fires.

THEO VON: Yeah. Do you start to develop a certain, like, attitudes towards humanity or drug users or society?

## Dealing with “Skells”

TONY BONFIGLIO: Absolutely. Call them skells. They’re all skells. Yeah, to us back then, you know, and matter of fact, on my block, we had a tight block, 161st Street, and we had a bodega, a gypsy cab dispatcher, a whorehouse and bunch of drug apartments.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And it was a busy block and we had two guys on the block that were almost there the whole time I was there and they were the lookouts. And one guy was bald and we called him Eight Ball and the other guy always had a hat and we called him The Hat.

And the funny part was they would watch our cars for us so nobody would break into the cars and shit. But, you know, I mean, I would see every agency there is come down and raid our block through the years I was there. ATF, Manhattan Tactical North, CIA. I mean, everything I tell you who was the worst ones was the FBI. Yeah, they come down with machine guns and I’ve never seen anybody that. And then they clear the street, they hit all the apartments. It’s amazing.

THEO VON: I bet they were all just visiting that whorehouse.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, I would inspect it now and then. I’d go in there with my hat and that thing and they’d all be looking at me and I’ll be like, what the f* you want? You know, like, I got to inspect this place.

THEO VON: You know, what was it like in some of those joints? Was it interesting? And there, was it just women trying to just survive? Most of those types of some middle.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Aged, young, not too young Dominican women, I guess they took care of the cab drivers and stuff. And they had the different rooms, you know, everybody had their little bed in there.

THEO VON: Just trying to.

TONY BONFIGLIO: And I would inspect it, you know, I got you. You got condoms? The condoms are here.

THEO VON: Making sure the alarm works, ladies.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah, they would be like, come on. Some of them were, the prostitutes would be like, get the f* out of here, man. You know, they’d be up all night. They didn’t want nothing to do with me.

## Hotel Fire Alarms

And I got to the point where every now, like, you know, every seven weeks, somebody’d start a fire, pull an alarm, right? And so in the middle of the night, you got to go downstairs and be outside hot. So I finally decided for myself, I’m going to wait because, yeah, I’m not going out there.

## The Coke Money Fire

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, plenty of drug dens. I mean, every building had a drug apartment where they would sell drugs. Oh, yeah, yeah. But this one fire that I went to, it was in the middle of the day. It was like the second floor, we pull up, fire’s blowing out four windows. I mean, it was going good.

And so we run out. Now we’re second due, which means we go to the floor above the fire. So the first due truck has the fire floor. Second due goes to the floor above, which the floor above is really shitty.

THEO VON: Because that’s where the heat’s going up.

TONY BONFIGLIO: That’s where everything’s going, you know. So now we get up the staircase and it’s starting to bank down and get dark. And I see a guy runs past me, no shirt on, no shoes. It’s got like a 3 year old kid under his arm and a Hefty bag.

THEO VON: Oh, yeah. So a politician probably.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So he slips by us, you know. So now we’re going to take the door in the hallway above the fire apartment and it’s getting smoky and my boss puts his mask on, but we have to take the door. So I wanted to see a little bit.

So I’m hitting the, you know, the guy’s got the halligan in the door jam and I’m smashing it with an ax and he’s trying to get a bite on the jam to bust the door open. So finally we get it, we bust the door open, we put our masks on, and we go in, and we crawling around.

And the engine company did a good job. They knocked the fire down pretty fast, you know? So as we’re crawling around, it’s starting. The black smoke starting to go away, and it’s getting lighter, and I’m on the floor, and I bump into a dresser.

Oh, now I could start to see a little bit. I put my light on the dresser. All the drawers are open. Stacks of hundreds and fifty dollar bills. Every drawer. I mean, no twenties. No. It was all coke money.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Bad. And I’m like, oh, my God. You know, I’m still like, I’m like my one year there. So I’m, oh, yeah, I’m a junior man.

THEO VON: Like, I got to get something for Christine.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Well, that’s it, you know, I got an angel and a devil on my shoulders, and they’re fighting it out. You know, I’m looking at, we’re in smoke. So, I mean, it ain’t like a setup. Nobody’s ever going to notice.

THEO VON: Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: So I’m thinking, oh, my God, I could buy myself a new Harley with that money, you know? And then I was like, the angel be like, oh, you buy the new Harley, and you’re going to crash, and you’ll get paralyzed. And I’m like, oh, shit. Yeah, it could happen.

So now I told the senior man, I said, Eddie, come here. Look in this dream drawers.

THEO VON: Yeah. Bring another guy in.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Senior man. You know, he’s like, holy Tony. He’s like, what are we going to do here?

THEO VON: Oh, Eddie’s setting you up again.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah. I’m like, you’re the senior man. You tell me.

THEO VON: This is dirty, Tennessee, guys.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I could have been stacking thousands in my pocket.

THEO VON: If Eddie would have been like, hey, let’s take a little. You have to.

TONY BONFIGLIO: I would. Yeah.

THEO VON: Yeah. You got to. I think, just to even make sure that it is what it is, get it back home.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Right. You know, I was young. I had this, oh, it’s dirty money. It’ll bring me bad juju. Yeah.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

## The Drug Bust Discovery

So the boss walks in, you know, the lieutenant, and he’s like, what do you got? And I was like, we got all this drawer full of mud, dresser full of money. He’s like, oh, I don’t see nothing. He was like, I don’t know. If you ever see Schultz on Hogan’s Heroes. He’s like, “I see nothing.” He turns around, he walks away, you know, like.

So now we go out, and it’s still a little smoky, but we could see we don’t have our masks on. And there’s another room with a padlock on the door. So he’s like, take the door. So he puts the ads in at the Halligan, and I whack it with the ax. Yeah, the door would bust it open.

And Theo, I’m telling you, there was like half the coke in Manhattan was in there. Bricks all the way to the ceiling on all sides of the walls. A pile of cocaine about 18 inches high on the table. We were like, holy s*. So the boss gets on. Tell the battalion we got a drug apartment. Send the PD up.

So now we’re, like, waiting for the PD to come. We wanted them to come before the drug dealers got back. So finally, two NYPD guys show up, and they’re like, yeah, guys, what do you got? I said, well, go in that room and go in that room. I said, that’s the money room. That’s the coke room. So they both go in, and at the same time, they go, holy s*.

THEO VON: Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: They call a backup, you know? So now we’re like. My boss is like, we’re out of here. You know, we don’t want me. Nothing. Part of this whole show here.

## The Changing Face of the Fire Department

And plus, back then, you know, they didn’t have computers and, you know, there was no cameras. It was BC. So you got away with a lot of.

TONY BONFIGLIO: If you f* up, people are seeing it right there.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Absolutely.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yes. So that’s a whole bit. And the other day, I was watching the chief had like, a some kind of iPad thing or something. And they had a drone in the air. No. And the thing was showing them all the roof, the holes, the fire, the back. It was like a 3D thing. Thing is, like, everything changes, you know?

THEO VON: Yeah. There’s already a real estate agent there. Yeah, everything’s changed.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Changes. So, you know, when I got on, the guys from the 60s and 70s, they were tough because.

THEO VON: So some of them come from, like, come from war, actually, and big time. And this was just another place where they at least had, like, camaraderie. They had a brotherhood.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Exactly. Because it was a paramilitary organization. So, you know, you had your lieutenants and your captains and your chiefs.

THEO VON: Did the stress of the job ever affect guys too much? Was there scenarios like that that kind of happened or not really?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Nah. I think most guys love the job.

THEO VON: What made you end up kind of loving it? Like, what made. Like, what kind of changed for you, like, or when you look back.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Brotherhood.

THEO VON: Really?

TONY BONFIGLIO: Oh, my God. Like family. Yeah. Yeah, that’s. That’s why, you know, I did 15 years in 34 truck from a probie. I did 15 years. I think I left there in 98. But yeah, I mean, I had a lot of friends, but I’m still today, you know, we’re all together. The wives, the kids. The kids are on the job. Some of them are already captains.

THEO VON: Wow.

TONY BONFIGLIO: Yeah.

THEO VON: Yeah, I guess that’s something that’s so nice is just that. That’s one thing that’s harder. I even notice about life, even just like, as you get out of times in your life where you’re either in school, we have, like, teams that you’re on or like your buddies are always around or in the college. You don’t really find a lot of places where there’s that much camaraderie anymore. You just don’t find it.

## Firehouse Meals and Celebrations

TONY BONFIGLIO: No. And that was it. That was it there, big time. We’d make the meals. Yeah. See, this is a. The two probies are coming off their probation. So when you come off probation, you throw a big party in the firehouse, and they all got lobsters and filet mignons, and they have a big celebration. It’s a lot. I remember my probing meal. But you know what, kids? You got to make it here next time. So I was like, I get it. You got to make the meal in the firehouse.

THEO VON: That’s part of it.
